/></a></td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<p><b>Columbia University</b> (<b>Columbia</b>; officially <b>Columbia University in the City of New York</b>), established in 1754, is a <a href="/wiki/Private_university" title="Private university">private</a> <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a> <a href="/wiki/Doctoral_university" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctoral university">research university</a> in <a href="/wiki/Upper_Manhattan" title="Upper Manhattan">Upper Manhattan</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, often cited as one of the world\'s most prestigious universities.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Columbia contains the oldest college in the state of <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> and is the fifth chartered institution of <a href="/wiki/Higher_learning" class="mw-redirect" title="Higher learning">higher learning</a> in the United States, making it <a href="/wiki/List_of_Colonial_Colleges" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Colonial Colleges">one of nine colonial colleges</a> founded prior to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> It was established as King\'s College by <a href="/wiki/Royal_charter" title="Royal charter">royal charter</a> of <a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Great_Britain" title="George II of Great Britain">George II of Great Britain</a> and renamed <a href="/wiki/Columbia_College,_Columbia_University" title="Columbia College, Columbia University">Columbia College</a> in 1784 following the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>. The college has produced numerous <a href="/wiki/List_of_Columbia_College_people" title="List of Columbia College people">distinguished alumni</a>. It has additionally been amongst the most selective colleges in the United States since its founding. With an undergraduate acceptance rate of 5.8%, it currently stands as the third most selective college in the United States and second most selective college in the Ivy League.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>\n<p>A 1787 charter placed the institution under a private board of trustees before it was renamed Columbia University in 1896 when the campus was moved from <a href="/wiki/Madison_Avenue" title="Madison Avenue">Madison Avenue</a> to its current location in <a href="/wiki/Morningside_Heights,_Manhattan" title="Morningside Heights, Manhattan">Morningside Heights</a> occupying 32 acres (13&#160;ha) of land.<sup id="cite_ref-A_Brief_History_of_Columbia_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Brief_History_of_Columbia-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-studentaffairs.columbia_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-studentaffairs.columbia-21">[21]</a></sup> Columbia is one of the fourteen founding members of the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Universities" title="Association of American Universities">Association of American Universities</a> and was the first school in the United States to grant the <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Medicine" title="Doctor of Medicine">M.D. degree</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-A_Brief_History_of_Columbia_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Brief_History_of_Columbia-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The university administers annually the <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The university is organized into twenty schools, including three undergraduate schools, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_College,_Columbia_University" title="Columbia College, Columbia University">Columbia College</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_School_of_Engineering_and_Applied_Science" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbia School of Engineering and Applied Science">School of Engineering and Applied Science</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_School_of_General_Studies" title="Columbia University School of General Studies">School of General Studies</a>, as well as graduate schools such as <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Law_School" title="Columbia Law School">Columbia Law School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_College_of_Physicians_and_Surgeons" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbia College of Physicians and Surgeons">Columbia College of Physicians and Surgeons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teachers_College,_Columbia_University" title="Teachers College, Columbia University">Teachers College</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Business_School" title="Columbia Business School">Columbia Business School</a>. The university also has <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Global_Centers" title="Columbia Global Centers">global research outposts</a> in <a href="/wiki/Amman" title="Amman">Amman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Santiago" title="Santiago">Santiago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asunci%C3%B3n" title="Asunci\xc3\xb3n">Asunci\xc3\xb3n</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nairobi" title="Nairobi">Nairobi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> It has affiliations with several other institutions nearby, including <a href="/wiki/List_College" title="List College">List College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barnard_College" title="Barnard College">Barnard College</a> and <a href="/wiki/Union_Theological_Seminary_in_the_City_of_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Union Theological Seminary in the City of New York">Union Theological Seminary</a>, with joint undergraduate programs available through the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Theological_Seminary_of_America" title="Jewish Theological Seminary of America">Jewish Theological Seminary of America</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_College_London" title="University College London">University College London</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sciences_Po" title="Sciences Po">Sciences Po</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/City_University_of_Hong_Kong" title="City University of Hong Kong">City University of Hong Kong</a><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Juilliard_School" title="Juilliard School">Juilliard School</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-college.columbia_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-college.columbia-28">[28]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The university has graduated many notable alumni. As of 2017, alumni and affiliates include 5 <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Fathers of the United States</a> \xe2\x80\x93 amongst these an <a href="/wiki/Robert_R._Livingston_(chancellor)" title="Robert R. Livingston (chancellor)">author</a> <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Five" title="Committee of Five">of the</a> <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_Independence_(U.S.)" class="mw-redirect" title="Declaration of Independence (U.S.)">Declaration of Independence</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Gouverneur_Morris" title="Gouverneur Morris">author</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a>; 10 Justices of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">United States Supreme Court</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_by_university_affiliation" title="List of Nobel laureates by university affiliation">96 Nobel laureates</a>; 20 living billionaires;<sup id="cite_ref-Marie_Thibault_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Thibault-30">[30]</a></sup> 123 <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a> winners; 39 <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a> winners;<sup id="cite_ref-Columbia_Arts_Alumni_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia_Arts_Alumni-31">[31]</a></sup> 3 <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">United States Presidents</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> 29 <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">heads of state</a>; 101 National Academy members;<sup id="cite_ref-Members_By_Parent_Institution_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Members_By_Parent_Institution-33">[33]</a></sup>77 <a href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Science_Laureate" class="mw-redirect" title="National Medal of Science Laureate">National Medal of Science winners</a>; and 23 <a href="/wiki/National_Humanities_Medal" title="National Humanities Medal">National Humanities Medal</a> recipients.</p>\n<p></p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc">\n<div class="toctitle">\n<h2>Contents</h2>\n</div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 <span class="toctext">Students</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-16"><a href="#Publications"><span class="tocnumber">4.1.1</span> <span activism</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Protests_of_1968"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Protests of 1968</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Protests_against_racism_and_apartheid"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Protests against racism and apartheid</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Ahmadinejad_speech_controversy"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Ahmadinejad speech controversy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#ROTC_controversy"><span class="tocnumber">5.4</span> <span class="toctext">ROTC controversy</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 <span class="toctext">Tree-Lighting and Yule Log ceremonies</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#The_Varsity_Show"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">The Varsity Show</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#Notable_people"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Notable people</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<p></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Columbia_University" title="History of Columbia University">History of Columbia University</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial">Colonial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Colonial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kings_college_1770.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Kings_college_1770.gif/220px-Kings_college_1770.gif" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Kings_college_1770.gif/330px-Kings_college_1770.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Kings_college_1770.gif/440px-Kings_college_1770.gif 2x" data-file-width="457" data-file-height="280" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kings_college_1770.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nKing\'s College Hall, 1770</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Discussions regarding the founding of a college in the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_New_York" title="Province of New York">Province of New York</a> began as early as 1704, at which time <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Morris_(governor)" title="Lewis Morris (governor)">Colonel Lewis Morris</a> wrote to the <a href="/wiki/USPG" title="USPG">Society for the Propagation of the Gospel in Foreign Parts</a>, the missionary arm of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>, persuading the society that New York City was an ideal community in which to establish a college.<sup id="cite_ref-McCaughey2003_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCaughey2003-34">[34]</a></sup> However, it was not until the founding of the College of New Jersey (the present <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a>) across the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_River" title="Hudson River">Hudson River</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> that the City of New York seriously consider founding a college.<sup id="cite_ref-McCaughey2003_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCaughey2003-34">[34]</a></sup> In 1746, an act was passed by the general assembly of New York to raise funds for the foundation of a new college. In 1751, the assembly appointed a commission of ten New York residents, seven of whom were members of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>, to direct the funds accrued by the <a href="/wiki/State_lottery" class="mw-redirect" title="State lottery">state lottery</a> towards the foundation of a college.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Classes were initially held in July 1754 and were presided over by the college\'s first president, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson_(American_educator)" title="Samuel Johnson (American educator)">Dr. Samuel Johnson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Dr. Johnson was the only instructor of the college\'s first class, which consisted of a mere eight students. Instruction was held in a new schoolhouse adjoining <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Church_(Manhattan)" title="Trinity Church (Manhattan)">Trinity Church</a>, located on what is now lower Broadway in Manhattan.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The college was officially founded on October 31, 1754, as King\'s College by royal charter of <a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Great_Britain" title="George II of Great Britain">King George II</a>, making it the oldest institution of higher learning in the state of New York and the fifth oldest in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-A_Brief_History_of_Columbia_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Brief_History_of_Columbia-20">[20]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 1763, Dr. Johnson was succeeded in the presidency by <a href="/wiki/Myles_Cooper" title="Myles Cooper">Myles Cooper</a>, a graduate of <a href="/wiki/The_Queen%27s_College,_Oxford" title="The Queen\'s College, Oxford">The Queen\'s College, Oxford</a>, and an ardent Tory. In the charged political climate of the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>, his chief opponent in discussions at the college was an undergraduate of the class of 1777, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> broke out in 1776, and was catastrophic for the operation of King\'s College, which suspended instruction for eight years beginning in 1776 with the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a>. The suspension continued through the military occupation of New York City by British troops until their <a href="/wiki/Evacuation_Day_(New_York)" title="Evacuation Day (New York)">departure</a> in 1783. The college\'s library was looted and its sole building requisitioned for use as a military hospital first by American and then British forces.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Loyalists were forced to abandon their King\'s College in New York, which was seized by the rebels and renamed Columbia College. The Loyalists, led by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Inglis_(bishop)" title="Charles Inglis (bishop)">Bishop Charles Inglis</a> fled to Windsor, Nova Scotia, where they founded <a href="/wiki/King%27s-Edgehill_School" title="King\'s-Edgehill School">King\'s Collegiate School</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LP_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LP-41">[41]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="18th_century">18th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 18th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Columbia_law_madison.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Columbia_law_madison.gif/170px-Columbia_law_madison.gif" width="170" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Columbia_law_madison.gif/255px-Columbia_law_madison.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/49/Columbia_law_madison.gif 2x" data-file-width="295" data-file-height="379" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Columbia_law_madison.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival" class="mw-redirect" title="Gothic Revival">Gothic Revival</a> Law School building on the Madison Avenue campus</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>After the Revolution, the college turned to the <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">State of New York</a> in order to restore its vitality, promising to make whatever changes to the school\'s charter the state might demand.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The Legislature agreed to assist the college, and on May 1, 1784, it passed "an Act for granting certain privileges to the College heretofore called King\'s College".<sup id="cite_ref-History_of_Columbia_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_Columbia-43">[43]</a></sup> The Act created a <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Regents" class="mw-redirect" title="Board of Regents">Board of Regents</a> to oversee the resuscitation of King\'s College, and, in an effort to demonstrate its support for the new Republic, the Legislature stipulated that "the College within the City of New York heretofore called King\'s College be forever hereafter called and known by the name of <a href="/wiki/Columbia_College_of_Columbia_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbia College of Columbia University">Columbia College</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-History_of_Columbia_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_Columbia-43">[43]</a></sup> a reference to <a href="/wiki/Columbia_(name)" title="Columbia (name)">Columbia</a>, an alternative name for America. The Regents finally became aware of the college\'s defective constitution in February 1787 and appointed a revision committee, which was headed by <a href="/wiki/John_Jay" title="John Jay">John Jay</a> and Alexander Hamilton. In April of that same year, a new charter was adopted for the college, still in use today, granting power to a private board of 24 Trustees.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>\n<p>On May 21, 1787, <a href="/wiki/William_Samuel_Johnson" title="William Samuel Johnson">William Samuel Johnson</a>, the son of <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson_(American_educator)" title="Samuel Johnson (American educator)">Dr. Samuel Johnson</a>, was unanimously elected President of Columbia College. Prior to serving at the university, Johnson had participated in the <a href="/wiki/First_Continental_Congress" title="First Continental Congress">First Continental Congress</a> and been chosen as a delegate to the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Convention (United States)">Constitutional Convention</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> For a period in the 1790s, with New York City as the federal and state capital and the country under successive <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Federalist Party (United States)">Federalist</a> governments, a revived Columbia thrived under the auspices of Federalists such as Hamilton and Jay. Both President <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> and Vice President <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> attended the college\'s commencement on May 6, 1789, as a tribute of honor to the many alumni of the school who had been involved in the American Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Detroit_Photographic_Company_(0671).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Detroit_Photographic_Company_%280671%29.jpg/220px-Detroit_Photographic_Company_%280671%29.jpg" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Detroit_Photographic_Company_%280671%29.jpg/330px-Detroit_Photographic_Company_%280671%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Detroit_Photographic_Company_%280671%29.jpg/440px-Detroit_Photographic_Company_%280671%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1191" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Detroit_Photographic_Company_(0671).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe Library at Columbia University, ca. 1900</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century_to_present">19th century to present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 19th century to present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Low_Memorial_Library_Columbia_University_NYC_retouched.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Low_Memorial_Library_Columbia_University_NYC_retouched.jpg/220px-Low_Memorial_Library_Columbia_University_NYC_retouched.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Low_Memorial_Library_Columbia_University_NYC_retouched.jpg/330px-Low_Memorial_Library_Columbia_University_NYC_retouched.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Low_Memorial_Library_Columbia_University_NYC_retouched.jpg/440px-Low_Memorial_Library_Columbia_University_NYC_retouched.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Low_Memorial_Library_Columbia_University_NYC_retouched.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Low_Memorial_Library" title="Low Memorial Library">Low Memorial Library</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>In November 1813, the College agreed to incorporate its medical school with The College of Physicians and Surgeons, a new school created by the Regents of New York, forming <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_College_of_Physicians_and_Surgeons" title="Columbia University College of Physicians and Surgeons">Columbia University College of Physicians and Surgeons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Moore_1846_53.E2.80.9360_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore_1846_53.E2.80.9360-2">[2]</a></sup> The college\'s enrollment, structure, and academics stagnated for the majority of the 19th century, with many of the college presidents doing little to change the way that the college functioned. In 1857, the college moved from the King\'s College campus at Park Place to a primarily <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival" class="mw-redirect" title="Gothic Revival">Gothic Revival</a> campus on 49th&#160;Street and <a href="/wiki/Madison_Avenue" title="Madison Avenue">Madison Avenue</a>, where it remained for the next forty years. During the last half of the 19th century, under the leadership of President <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Augustus_Porter_Barnard" title="Frederick Augustus Porter Barnard">F.A.P. Barnard</a>, the institution rapidly assumed the shape of a modern university.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> By this time, the college\'s investments in New York real estate became a primary source of steady income for the school, mainly owing to the city\'s expanding population.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> University president <a href="/wiki/Seth_Low" title="Seth Low">Seth Low</a> moved the campus from 49th&#160;Street to its present location, a more spacious campus in the developing neighborhood of <a href="/wiki/Morningside_Heights" class="mw-redirect" title="Morningside Heights">Morningside Heights</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Under the leadership of Low\'s successor, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Murray_Butler" title="Nicholas Murray Butler">Nicholas Murray Butler</a>, who served for over four decades, Columbia rapidly became the nation\'s major institution for research, setting the "multiversity" model that later universities would adopt.<sup id="cite_ref-A_Brief_History_of_Columbia_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Brief_History_of_Columbia-20">[20]</a></sup> Prior to becoming the president of Columbia University, Butler founded <a href="/wiki/Teachers_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Teachers College">Teachers College</a>, as a school to prepare home economists and manual art teachers for the children of the poor, with philanthropist <a href="/wiki/Grace_Hoadley_Dodge" title="Grace Hoadley Dodge">Grace Hoadley Dodge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-stand_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stand-50">[50]</a></sup> Teachers College came under the aegis of Columbia University in 1893 and became the University\'s Graduate School of Education.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-51">[51]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Research into the atom by faculty members <a href="/wiki/John_R._Dunning" title="John R. Dunning">John R. Dunning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isidor_Isaac_Rabi" title="Isidor Isaac Rabi">I. I. Rabi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Fermi" title="Enrico Fermi">Enrico Fermi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polykarp_Kusch" title="Polykarp Kusch">Polykarp Kusch</a> placed Columbia\'s Physics Department in the international spotlight in the 1940s after the first nuclear pile was built to start what became the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Broad_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broad-52">[52]</a></sup> In 1928, <a href="/wiki/Seth_Low" title="Seth Low">Seth Low</a> Junior College was established by Columbia University in order to mitigate the number of Jewish applicants to Columbia College.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The college was closed in 1938 due to the adverse effects of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> and its students were subsequently absorbed into University Extension.<sup id="cite_ref-spectatorarchive.library.columbia.edu_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spectatorarchive.library.columbia.edu-54">[54]</a></sup> In 1947, the program was reorganized as an undergraduate college and designated the <a href="/wiki/School_of_General_Studies" class="mw-redirect" title="School of General Studies">School of General Studies</a> in response to the return of <a href="/wiki/GI_(military)" class="mw-redirect" title="GI (military)">GIs</a> after World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> In 1995, the School of General Studies was again reorganized as a full-fledged liberal arts college for non-traditional students (those who have had an academic break of one year or more, or are pursuing dual-degrees) and was fully integrated into Columbia\'s traditional undergraduate curriculum.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Within the same year, the Division of Special Programs\xe2\x80\x94later the School of Continuing Education, and now the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_School_of_Professional_Studies" title="Columbia University School of Professional Studies">School of Professional Studies</a>\xe2\x80\x94was established to reprise the former role of University Extension.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> While the School of Professional Studies only offered non-degree programs for lifelong learners and high school students in its earliest stages, it now offers degree programs in a diverse range of professional and inter-disciplinary fields.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Almamater.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Almamater.jpg/220px-Almamater.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Almamater.jpg/330px-Almamater.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Almamater.jpg/440px-Almamater.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="759" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Almamater.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<i><a href="/wiki/Alma_Mater_(New_York_sculpture)" title="Alma Mater (New York sculpture)">Alma Mater</a></i></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>In the aftermath of World War II, the discipline of international relations became a major scholarly focus of the University, and in response, the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_School_of_International_and_Public_Affairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbia University School of International and Public Affairs">School of International and Public Affairs</a> was founded in 1946, drawing upon the resources of the faculties of political science, economics, and history.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>\n<p>During the 1960s <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_protests_of_1968" title="Columbia University protests of 1968">Columbia experienced large-scale student activism</a>, which reached a climax in the spring of 1968 when hundreds of students occupied buildings on campus. The incident forced the resignation of Columbia\'s President, <a href="/wiki/Grayson_Kirk" class="mw-redirect" title="Grayson Kirk">Grayson Kirk</a> and the establishment of the University Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Though several schools within the university had admitted women for years, Columbia College first admitted women in the fall of 1983, after a decade of failed negotiations with <a href="/wiki/Barnard_College" title="Barnard College">Barnard College</a>, the all-female institution affiliated with the university, to merge the two schools.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Barnard College still remains affiliated with Columbia, and all Barnard graduates are issued diplomas authorized by both Columbia University and Barnard College.<sup id="cite_ref-test_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-test-63">[63]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In the 1970s, Teachers College\'s departments ran joint Ph.D programs with the University in the disciplines of psychology, history, and anthropology and all Teachers College diplomas were conferred by Columbia University.<sup id="cite_ref-stand_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stand-50">[50]</a></sup> Teachers College was soon established as the University\'s Faculty of Education and Department of Education.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-51">[51]</a></sup></p>\n<p>During the late 20th century, the University underwent significant academic, structural, and administrative changes as it developed into a major research university. For much of the 19th century, the University consisted of decentralized and separate faculties specializing in Political Science, Philosophy, and Pure Science. In 1979, these faculties were merged into the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Graduate_School_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="Columbia Graduate School of Arts and Sciences">Graduate School of Arts and Sciences</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In 1991, the faculties of Columbia College, the School of General Studies, the Graduate School of Arts and Sciences, the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_School_of_the_Arts" title="Columbia University School of the Arts">School of the Arts</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_School_of_Professional_Studies" title="Columbia University School of Professional Studies">School of Professional Studies</a> were merged into the Faculty of Arts and Sciences, leading to the academic integration and centralized governance of these schools. In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_School_of_International_and_Public_Affairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbia University School of International and Public Affairs">School of International and Public Affairs</a>, which was previously a part of the Faculty of Arts and Sciences, became an independent faculty.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campus">Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Campus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>According to <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_(magazine)" title="New York (magazine)">New York</a></i>, Columbia University is the second largest landowner in New York City, after the Catholic Church.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morningside_Heights">Morningside Heights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Morningside Heights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Columbia_College_Walk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Columbia_College_Walk.jpg/220px-Columbia_College_Walk.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Columbia_College_Walk.jpg/330px-Columbia_College_Walk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Columbia_College_Walk.jpg/440px-Columbia_College_Walk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Columbia_College_Walk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nCollege Walk</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The majority of Columbia\'s graduate and undergraduate studies are conducted in <a href="/wiki/Morningside_Heights,_Manhattan" title="Morningside Heights, Manhattan">Morningside Heights</a> on <a href="/wiki/Seth_Low" title="Seth Low">Seth Low</a>\'s late-19th century vision of a university campus where all disciplines could be taught at one location. The campus was designed along <a href="/wiki/Beaux-Arts_architecture" title="Beaux-Arts architecture">Beaux-Arts</a> principles by architects <a href="/wiki/McKim,_Mead,_and_White" class="mw-redirect" title="McKim, Mead, and White">McKim, Mead, and White</a>. Columbia\'s main campus occupies more than six <a href="/wiki/City_block" title="City block">city blocks</a>, or 32 acres (13&#160;ha), in <a href="/wiki/Morningside_Heights" class="mw-redirect" title="Morningside Heights">Morningside Heights</a>, New York City, a neighborhood that contains a number of academic institutions. The university owns over 7,800 apartments in Morningside Heights, housing faculty, graduate students, and staff. Almost two dozen undergraduate dormitories (purpose-built or converted) are located on campus or in Morningside Heights. Columbia University has an extensive <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_tunnels" title="Columbia University tunnels">underground tunnel system</a> more than a century old, with the oldest portions predating the present campus. Some of these remain accessible to the public, while others have been cordoned off.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Butler_Library_-_1000px_-_AC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Butler_Library_-_1000px_-_AC.jpg/220px-Butler_Library_-_1000px_-_AC.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Butler_Library_-_1000px_-_AC.jpg/330px-Butler_Library_-_1000px_-_AC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Butler_Library_-_1000px_-_AC.jpg/440px-Butler_Library_-_1000px_-_AC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="664" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Butler_Library_-_1000px_-_AC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Butler_Library" title="Butler Library">Butler Library</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The Nicholas Murray Butler Library, commonly known simply as <a href="/wiki/Butler_Library" title="Butler Library">Butler Library</a>, is the largest single library in the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_Library_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbia University Library System">Columbia University Library System</a>, and is one of the largest buildings on the campus. Proposed as "South Hall" by the university\'s former President <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Murray_Butler" title="Nicholas Murray Butler">Nicholas Murray Butler</a> as expansion plans for <a href="/wiki/Low_Memorial_Library" title="Low Memorial Library">Low Memorial Library</a> stalled, the new library was funded by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Harkness" title="Edward Harkness">Edward Harkness</a>, benefactor of Yale\'s <a href="/wiki/Residential_college" title="Residential college">residential college</a> system, and designed by his favorite architect, <a href="/wiki/James_Gamble_Rogers" title="James Gamble Rogers">James Gamble Rogers</a>. It was completed in 1934 and renamed for Butler in 1946. The library design is <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">neo-classical</a> in style. Its facade features a row of columns in the <a href="/wiki/Ionic_order" title="Ionic order">Ionic order</a> above which are inscribed the names of great writers, philosophers, and thinkers, most of whom are read by students engaged in the <a href="/wiki/Core_Curriculum_(Columbia_College)" title="Core Curriculum (Columbia College)">Core Curriculum</a> of <a href="/wiki/Columbia_College_of_Columbia_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbia College of Columbia University">Columbia College</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> As of 2012, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_Library_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbia University Library System">Columbia\'s library system</a> includes over 11.9 &#160;million volumes, making it the eighth largest library system and fifth largest collegiate library system in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> It has also been ranked among the United States\' most beautiful libraries.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Teachers_College_today.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Teachers_College_today.JPG/220px-Teachers_College_today.JPG" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Teachers_College_today.JPG/330px-Teachers_College_today.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/51/Teachers_College_today.JPG 2x" data-file-width="438" data-file-height="323" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Teachers_College_today.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Teachers_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Teachers College">Teachers College</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Several buildings on the Morningside Heights campus are listed on the <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a>. <a href="/wiki/Low_Memorial_Library" title="Low Memorial Library">Low Memorial Library</a>, a <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark" title="National Historic Landmark">National Historic Landmark</a> and the centerpiece of the campus, is listed for its architectural significance. <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_Hall" title="Philosophy Hall">Philosophy Hall</a> is listed as the site of the invention of <a href="/wiki/FM_radio" class="mw-redirect" title="FM radio">FM radio</a>. Also listed is <a href="/wiki/Pupin_Hall" title="Pupin Hall">Pupin Hall</a>, another <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark" title="National Historic Landmark">National Historic Landmark</a>, which houses the physics and astronomy departments. Here the first experiments on the fission of uranium were conducted by <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Fermi" title="Enrico Fermi">Enrico Fermi</a>. The uranium atom was split there ten days after the world\'s first atom-splitting in <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen,_Denmark" class="mw-redirect" title="Copenhagen, Denmark">Copenhagen, Denmark</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Union_Theological_Seminary_NYC_001_002_combined.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Union_Theological_Seminary_NYC_001_002_combined.jpg/220px-Union_Theological_Seminary_NYC_001_002_combined.jpg" width="220" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Union_Theological_Seminary_NYC_001_002_combined.jpg/330px-Union_Theological_Seminary_NYC_001_002_combined.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Union_Theological_Seminary_NYC_001_002_combined.jpg/440px-Union_Theological_Seminary_NYC_001_002_combined.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2468" data-file-height="2572" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Union_Theological_Seminary_NYC_001_002_combined.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Union_Theological_Seminary_in_the_City_of_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Union Theological Seminary in the City of New York">Union Theological Seminary</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>A statue by sculptor <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Chester_French" title="Daniel Chester French">Daniel Chester French</a> called <i><a href="/wiki/Alma_Mater_(New_York_sculpture)" title="Alma Mater (New York sculpture)">Alma Mater</a></i> is centered on the front steps of <a href="/wiki/Low_Memorial_Library" title="Low Memorial Library">Low Memorial Library</a>. McKim, Mead &amp; White invited French to build the sculpture in order to harmonize with the larger composition of the court and library in the center of the campus. Draped in an academic gown, the female figure of Alma Mater wears a crown of laurels and sits on a throne. The scroll-like arms of the throne end in lamps, representing <a href="/wiki/List_of_Latin_phrases_(S)" title="List of Latin phrases (S)">sapientia and doctrina</a>. A book signifying knowledge, balances on her lap, and an owl, the attribute of wisdom, is hidden in the folds of her gown. Her right hand holds a scepter composed of four sprays of wheat, terminating with a crown of King\'s College which refers to Columbia\'s origin as a Royalist institution in 1754. A local actress named Mary Lawton was said to have posed for parts of the sculpture. The statue was dedicated on September 23, 1903, as a gift of Mr. &amp; Mrs. Robert Goelet, and was originally covered in golden leaf. During the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_protests_of_1968" title="Columbia University protests of 1968">Columbia University protests of 1968</a> a bomb damaged the sculpture, but it has since been repaired.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The small hidden owl on the sculpture is also the subject of many Columbia legends, the main legend being that the first student in the freshmen class to find the hidden owl on the statue will be valedictorian, and that any subsequent Columbia male who finds it will marry a Barnard student, given that Barnard is a <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_college" title="Women\'s college">women\'s college</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>\n<p>"The Steps", alternatively known as "Low Steps" or the "Urban Beach", are a popular meeting area for Columbia students. The term refers to the long series of granite steps leading from the lower part of campus (South Field) to its upper terrace. With a design inspired by the <a href="/wiki/City_Beautiful_movement" title="City Beautiful movement">City Beautiful movement</a>, the steps of Low Library provides Columbia University and Barnard College students, faculty, and staff with a comfortable outdoor platform and space for informal gatherings, events, and ceremonies. McKim\'s classical facade epitomizes late 19th century new-classical designs, with its columns and portico marking the entrance to an important structure.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> On warm days when the weather is favorable, the Low Steps often become a popular gathering place for students to sunbathe, eat lunch, or play frisbee.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:1208px;">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="">\n<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Columbia_pano.jpg" class="image" title="Panoramic view of the Morningside Heights campus as seen from Butler Library and facing Low Memorial Library"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Columbia_pano.jpg/1200px-Columbia_pano.jpg" width="1200" height="193" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Columbia_pano.jpg/1800px-Columbia_pano.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Columbia_pano.jpg/2400px-Columbia_pano.jpg 2x" data-file-width="13687" data-file-height="2199" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:center">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Columbia_pano.jpg" title="File:Columbia pano.jpg"></a></div>\nPanoramic view of the Morningside Heights campus as seen from Butler Library and facing Low Memorial Library</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_campuses">Other campuses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Other campuses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LDEO_Entrance.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/LDEO_Entrance.jpg/220px-LDEO_Entrance.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/LDEO_Entrance.jpg/330px-LDEO_Entrance.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/LDEO_Entrance.jpg/440px-LDEO_Entrance.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LDEO_Entrance.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nLamont Campus entrance in <a href="/wiki/Palisades,_New_York" title="Palisades, New York">Palisades</a>, New York</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ColumbiaMedicalCenter.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/ColumbiaMedicalCenter.jpeg/220px-ColumbiaMedicalCenter.jpeg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/ColumbiaMedicalCenter.jpeg/330px-ColumbiaMedicalCenter.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/ColumbiaMedicalCenter.jpeg/440px-ColumbiaMedicalCenter.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="469" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ColumbiaMedicalCenter.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nMedical Center in <a href="/wiki/Washington_Heights,_Manhattan" title="Washington Heights, Manhattan">Washington Heights</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>In April 2007, the university purchased more than two-thirds of a 17 acres (6.9&#160;ha) site for a new campus in <a href="/wiki/Manhattanville" class="mw-redirect" title="Manhattanville">Manhattanville</a>, an industrial neighborhood to the north of the Morningside Heights campus. Stretching from <a href="/wiki/125th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="125th Street (Manhattan)">125th Street</a> to 133rd&#160;Street, the new campus will house buildings for Columbia\'s Business School, School of International and Public Affairs, and the Jerome L. Greene Center for Mind, Brain, and Behavior, where research will occur on neurodegenerative diseases such as Parkinson\'s and Alzheimer\'s.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The $7&#160;billion expansion plan includes demolishing all buildings, except three that are historically significant, eliminating the existing light industry and storage warehouses, and relocating tenants in 132&#160;apartments. Replacing these buildings will be 6,800,000 square feet (630,000&#160;m<sup>2</sup>) of space for the university. Community activist groups in West Harlem fought the expansion for reasons ranging from property protection and fair exchange for land, to residents\' rights.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Subsequent public hearings drew neighborhood opposition. Most recently (as of December 2008), the State of New York\'s <a href="/wiki/Empire_State_Development_Corporation" title="Empire State Development Corporation">Empire State Development Corporation</a> approved use of eminent domain, which, through declaration of Manhattanville\'s "blighted" status, gives governmental bodies the right to appropriate private property for public use.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> On May 20, 2009, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Public_Authorities_Control_Board" title="New York State Public Authorities Control Board">New York State Public Authorities Control Board</a> approved the Manhanttanville expansion plan and the first buildings are under construction.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/New_York-Presbyterian_Hospital" class="mw-redirect" title="New York-Presbyterian Hospital">New York-Presbyterian Hospital</a> is affiliated with the medical schools of both Columbia University and <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a>. According to <i>U.S. News &amp; World Report</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s "America\'s Best Hospitals 2009", it is ranked sixth overall and third among university hospitals. Columbia\'s <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_College_of_Physicians_and_Surgeons" title="Columbia University College of Physicians and Surgeons">medical school</a> has a strategic partnership with <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Psychiatric_Institute" title="New York State Psychiatric Institute">New York State Psychiatric Institute</a>, and is affiliated with 19 other hospitals in the U.S. and four hospitals overseas. Health-related schools are located at the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_Medical_Center" title="Columbia University Medical Center">Columbia University Medical Center</a>, a 20 acres (8.1&#160;ha) campus located in the neighborhood of <a href="/wiki/Washington_Heights,_Manhattan" title="Washington Heights, Manhattan">Washington Heights</a>, fifty blocks uptown. Other teaching hospitals affiliated with Columbia through the New York-Presbyterian network include the Payne Whitney Clinic in Manhattan, and the Payne Whitney Westchester, a psychiatric institute located in White Plains, New York.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> On the northern tip of Manhattan island (in the neighborhood of <a href="/wiki/Inwood,_Manhattan" title="Inwood, Manhattan">Inwood</a>), Columbia owns 26-acre (11&#160;ha) Baker Field, which includes the <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_A._Wien_Stadium" class="mw-redirect" title="Lawrence A. Wien Stadium">Lawrence A. Wien Stadium</a> as well as facilities for field sports, outdoor track, and tennis. There is a third campus on the west bank of the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_River" title="Hudson River">Hudson River</a>, the 157-acre (64&#160;ha) <a href="/wiki/Lamont-Doherty_Earth_Observatory" class="mw-redirect" title="Lamont-Doherty Earth Observatory">Lamont-Doherty Earth Observatory</a> and Earth Institute in <a href="/wiki/Palisades,_New_York" title="Palisades, New York">Palisades</a>, New York. A fourth is the 60-acre (24&#160;ha) <a href="/wiki/Nevis_Laboratories" title="Nevis Laboratories">Nevis Laboratories</a> in <a href="/wiki/Irvington,_New_York" title="Irvington, New York">Irvington</a>, New York for the study of particle and motion physics. A satellite site in Paris, France holds classes at <a href="/wiki/Reid_Hall" title="Reid Hall">Reid Hall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-A_Brief_History_of_Columbia_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Brief_History_of_Columbia-20">[20]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainability">Sustainability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Sustainability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Avery_Building_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0944.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Avery_Building_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0944.JPG/220px-Avery_Building_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0944.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Avery_Building_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0944.JPG/330px-Avery_Building_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0944.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Avery_Building_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0944.JPG/440px-Avery_Building_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0944.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Avery_Building_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0944.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Avery_Architectural_and_Fine_Arts_Library" title="Avery Architectural and Fine Arts Library">Avery Architectural and Fine Arts Library</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>In 2006, the university established the Office of Environmental Stewardship to initiate, coordinate and implement programs to reduce the university\'s environmental footprint. The U.S. Green Building Council selected the university\'s Manhattanville plan for the <a href="/wiki/Leadership_in_Energy_and_Environmental_Design" title="Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design">Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design</a> (LEED) Neighborhood Design pilot program. The plan commits to incorporating smart growth, new urbanism and "green" building design principles.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Columbia is one of the 2030 Challenge Partners, a group of nine universities in the city of New York that have pledged to reduce their greenhouse emissions by 30% within the next ten years. Columbia University adopts LEED standards for all new construction and major renovations. The University requires a minimum of Silver, but through its design and review process seeks to achieve higher levels. This is especially challenging for lab and research buildings with their intensive energy use; however, the university also uses lab design guidelines that seek to maximize energy efficiency while protecting the safety of researchers.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Every Thursday and Sunday of the month, Columbia hosts a <a href="/wiki/Farmers%27_market" title="Farmers\' market">greenmarket</a> where local farmers can sell their produce to residents of the city. In addition, from April to November Hodgson\'s farm, a local New York gardening center, joins the market bringing a large selection of plants and blooming flowers. The market is one of the many operated at different points throughout the city by the non-profit group GrowNYC.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Dining services at Columbia spends 36 percent of its food budget on local products, in addition to serving sustainably harvested seafood and fair trade coffee on campus.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Columbia has been rated "B+" by the 2011 College Sustainability Report Card for its environmental and sustainability initiatives.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics">Academics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Academics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Undergraduate_admissions_and_financial_aid">Undergraduate admissions and financial aid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Undergraduate admissions and financial aid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vanamquad.JPEG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Vanamquad.JPEG/220px-Vanamquad.JPEG" width="220" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Vanamquad.JPEG/330px-Vanamquad.JPEG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Vanamquad.JPEG/440px-Vanamquad.JPEG 2x" data-file-width="655" data-file-height="766" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vanamquad.JPEG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nVan Am Quad</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ColumbiaUndergraduateLogos.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/ColumbiaUndergraduateLogos.jpg/220px-ColumbiaUndergraduateLogos.jpg" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/ColumbiaUndergraduateLogos.jpg/330px-ColumbiaUndergraduateLogos.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/ColumbiaUndergraduateLogos.jpg/440px-ColumbiaUndergraduateLogos.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="250" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ColumbiaUndergraduateLogos.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nCollege (left), SEAS (right)</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Columbia University received 36,292 applications for its undergraduate class of 2020 (entering 2016). In early decision, 620 out of 3,520 applicants were admitted, for an acceptance rate of 17.61%. In regular decision, 1,573 out of 32,772 applicants were admitted, for an acceptance rate of 4.79%. For the Class of 2021, 2,185 out of 37,389 applicants were admitted for an overall acceptance rate of 5.8%, making Columbia the third most selective college in the United States behind <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard</a> and the 2nd most selective college in the Ivy League.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> According to the 2012 college selectivity ranking by <a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a>, which factors admission and yield rates among other criteria, Columbia was tied with <a href="/wiki/Yale" class="mw-redirect" title="Yale">Yale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caltech" class="mw-redirect" title="Caltech">Caltech</a> and <a href="/wiki/MIT" class="mw-redirect" title="MIT">MIT</a> as the most selective colleges in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Columbia is a racially diverse school, with approximately 52% of all students identifying themselves as persons of color. Additionally, 50% of all undergraduates received grants from Columbia. The average grant size awarded to these students is $46,516.<sup id="cite_ref-stat_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stat-94">[94]</a></sup> In 2015\xe2\x80\x932016, annual undergraduate tuition at Columbia was $50,526 with a total cost of attendance of $65,860 (including room and board).<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>\n<p>On April 11, 2007, Columbia University announced a $400m to $600m donation from media billionaire alumnus <a href="/wiki/John_Kluge" title="John Kluge">John Kluge</a> to be used exclusively for undergraduate financial aid. The donation is among the largest single gifts to higher education. Its exact value will depend on the eventual value of Kluge\'s estate at the time of his death; however, the generous donation has helped change financial aid policy at Columbia.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Annual gifts, fund-raising, and an increase in spending from the university\'s endowment have allowed Columbia to extend generous financial aid packages to qualifying students. As of 2008, undergraduates from families with incomes as high as $60,000 a year will have the projected cost of attending the university, including room, board, and academic fees, fully paid for by the university. That same year, the university ended loans for incoming and current students who were on financial aid, replacing loans that were traditionally part of aid packages with grants from the university. However, this does not apply to international students, transfer students, visiting students, or students in the School of General Studies.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> In the fall of 2010, admission to Columbia\'s undergraduate colleges <a href="/wiki/Columbia_College,_Columbia_University" title="Columbia College, Columbia University">Columbia College</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_School_of_Engineering_and_Applied_Science" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbia School of Engineering and Applied Science">Fu Foundation School of Engineering and Applied Science</a> (also known as SEAS or Columbia Engineering) began accepting the <a href="/wiki/Common_Application" title="Common Application">Common Application</a>. The policy change made Columbia one of the last major academic institutions and the last <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a> university to switch to the Common Application.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Scholarships are also given to undergraduate students by the admissions committee. Designations include John W. Kluge Scholars, John Jay Scholars, C. Prescott Davis Scholars, Global Scholars, Egleston Scholars, and Science Research Fellows. Named scholars are selected by the admission committee from first-year applicants. According to Columbia, the first four designated scholars "distinguish themselves for their remarkable academic and personal achievements, dynamism, intellectual curiosity, the originality and independence of their thinking, and the diversity that stems from their different cultures and their varied educational experiences."<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization">Organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a Studies">School of Professional Studies</a></td>\n<td>1995</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scholars_Lion.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Scholars_Lion.JPG/220px-Scholars_Lion.JPG" width="220" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Scholars_Lion.JPG/330px-Scholars_Lion.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Scholars_Lion.JPG/440px-Scholars_Lion.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1082" data-file-height="1157" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scholars_Lion.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<i>Scholars\' Lion</i>, Greg Wyatt (2004)</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Columbia University is an independent, privately supported, nonsectarian institution of higher education. Its official corporate name is "The Trustees of Columbia University in the City of New York." The university\'s first Charter was granted in 1754 by King George II; however, its modern Charter was first enacted in 1787 and last amended in 1810 by the New York State Legislature. The university is governed by 24 Trustees, customarily including the President, who serves <a href="/wiki/Ex_officio_member" title="Ex officio member">ex officio</a>. The Trustees themselves are responsible for choosing their successors. Six of the 24 are nominated from a pool of candidates recommended by the Columbia Alumni Association. Another six are nominated by the Board in consultation with the Executive Committee of the University Senate. The remaining 12, including the President, are nominated by the Trustees themselves through their internal processes. The term of office for Trustees is six years. Generally, they serve for no more than two consecutive terms. The Trustees appoint the President and other senior administrative officers of the university, and review and confirm faculty appointments as required. They determine the university\'s financial and investment policies, authorize the budget, supervise the endowment, direct the management of the university\'s real estate and other assets, and otherwise oversee the administration and management of the university.<sup id="cite_ref-Columbia.edu_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia.edu-101">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The University Senate was established by the Trustees after a university-wide referendum in 1969. It succeeded to the powers of the University Council, which was created in 1890 as a body of faculty, deans, and other administrators to regulate inter-Faculty affairs and consider issues of university-wide concern. The University Senate is a unicameral body consisting of 107 members drawn from all constituencies of the university. These include the president of the university, the Provost, the Deans of Columbia College and the Graduate School of Arts and Sciences, all who serve ex officio, and five additional representatives, appointed by the President, from the university\'s administration. The President serves as the Senate\'s presiding officer. The Senate is charged with reviewing the educational policies, physical development, budget, and external relations of the university. It oversees the welfare and academic freedom of the faculty and the welfare of students.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The Columbia University Senate includes faculty and student representatives from Teachers College and Barnard College who serve two-year terms; all senators are accorded full voting privileges regarding matters impacting the entire University.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_Columbia_University" title="List of Presidents of Columbia University">The President of Columbia University</a>, who is selected by the Trustees in consultation with the Executive Committee of the University Senate and who serves at the Trustees\' pleasure, is the chief executive officer of the university. Assisting the President in administering the University are the Provost, the Senior Executive Vice President, the Executive Vice President for Health and Biomedical Sciences, several other vice presidents, the General Counsel, the Secretary of the University, and the deans of the Faculties, all of whom are appointed by the Trustees on the nomination of the President and serve at their pleasure.<sup id="cite_ref-Columbia.edu_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia.edu-101">[101]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lee_C._Bollinger" class="mw-redirect" title="Lee C. Bollinger">Lee C. Bollinger</a> became the 19th President of Columbia University on June 1, 2002. A prominent advocate of affirmative action, he played a leading role in the twin Supreme Court cases\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Grutter_v_Bollinger" class="mw-redirect" title="Grutter v Bollinger">Grutter v Bollinger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gratz_v_Bollinger" class="mw-redirect" title="Gratz v Bollinger">Gratz v Bollinger</a>\xe2\x80\x94that upheld and clarified the importance of diversity as a compelling justification for affirmative action in higher education. A leading First Amendment scholar, he is widely published on freedom of speech and press, and<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (April 2012)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> serves on the faculty of Columbia Law School.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Columbia has three official undergraduate colleges: <a href="/wiki/Columbia_College_of_Columbia_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbia College of Columbia University">Columbia College (CC)</a>, the liberal arts college offering the Bachelor of Arts degree, the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_School_of_Engineering_and_Applied_Science" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbia School of Engineering and Applied Science">Fu Foundation School of Engineering and Applied Science</a> (also known as SEAS or Columbia Engineering) is the engineering and applied science school offering the Bachelor of Science degree, and <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_School_of_General_Studies" title="Columbia University School of General Studies">The School of General Studies (GS)</a>, the liberal arts college offering the Bachelor of Arts degree to non-traditional students undertaking full- or part-time study.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The university is affiliated with <a href="/wiki/Teachers_College,_Columbia_University" title="Teachers College, Columbia University">Teachers College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barnard_College" title="Barnard College">Barnard College</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Union_Theological_Seminary_in_the_City_of_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Union Theological Seminary in the City of New York">Union Theological Seminary</a>, all located nearby in Morningside Heights. Of these, Barnard College and Teachers College are integrated as Faculties of the university.<sup id="cite_ref-Columbia.edu_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia.edu-101">[101]</a></sup> Furthermore, Teachers College is incorporated as an academic department of Columbia University, serving as the University\'s Graduate School of Education and Department of Education.<sup id="cite_ref-Columbia.edu_101-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia.edu-101">[101]</a></sup> Joint undergraduate programs are available through the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Theological_Seminary_of_America" title="Jewish Theological Seminary of America">Jewish Theological Seminary of America</a> as well as through the <a href="/wiki/Juilliard_School" title="Juilliard School">Juilliard School</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rankings">Rankings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Rankings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div>\n<table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; Animal Science</td>\n<td>210</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Psychiatry/Psychology</td>\n<td>6</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Social Sciences &amp; Public Health</td>\n<td>8</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Space Science</td>\n<td>32</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Paul_Chapel_Columbia_jeh.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Paul_Chapel_Columbia_jeh.JPG/220px-Paul_Chapel_Columbia_jeh.JPG" width="220" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Paul_Chapel_Columbia_jeh.JPG/330px-Paul_Chapel_Columbia_jeh.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Paul_Chapel_Columbia_jeh.JPG/440px-Paul_Chapel_Columbia_jeh.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3120" data-file-height="2890" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Paul_Chapel_Columbia_jeh.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/St._Paul%27s_Chapel_(Columbia_University)" title="St. Paul\'s Chapel (Columbia University)">St. Paul\'s Chapel</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Columbia University was ranked 2nd among U.S. colleges for 2017 by Wall Street Journal/Times Higher Education and 2nd among Ivy League schools<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> was ranked 4th overall among U.S. national universities for 2016 by <i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Individual colleges and schools were also nationally ranked by <i>U.S. News &amp; World Report</i> for its 2016 edition. The <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Law_School" title="Columbia Law School">Columbia Law School</a> was ranked tied for 4th, the <a href="/wiki/Mailman_School_of_Public_Health" class="mw-redirect" title="Mailman School of Public Health">Mailman School of Public Health</a> 5th, the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_School_of_Social_Work" title="Columbia University School of Social Work">School
