of Social Work</a> 5th, the <a href="/wiki/Teachers_College,_Columbia_University" title="Teachers College, Columbia University">Teachers College</a> (Columbia Graduate School of Education) 7th, the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Business_School" title="Columbia Business School">Columbia Business School</a> 8th, the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_College_of_Physicians_and_Surgeons" title="Columbia University College of Physicians and Surgeons">College of Physicians and Surgeons</a> tied for 8th for research (and tied for 52nd for primary care), the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_School_of_the_Arts" title="Columbia University School of the Arts">Graduate School of Arts</a> 10th, the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_School_of_Nursing" title="Columbia University School of Nursing">School of Nursing</a> tied for 11th, and the <a href="/wiki/Fu_Foundation_School_of_Engineering_and_Applied_Science" title="Fu Foundation School of Engineering and Applied Science">Fu Foundation School of Engineering and Applied Science</a> (graduate) was ranked 14th.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 2016, Columbia was ranked 9th in the world by <i><a href="/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities" title="Academic Ranking of World Universities">Academic Ranking of World Universities</a></i>, 20th in the world by <i><a href="/wiki/QS_World_University_Rankings" title="QS World University Rankings">QS World University Rankings</a></i>, and 16th globally by <i><a href="/wiki/Times_Higher_Education_World_University_Rankings" title="Times Higher Education World University Rankings">Times Higher Education World University Rankings</a></i>.</p>\n<p>Rankings by other organizations include the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Graduate_School_of_Architecture,_Planning_and_Preservation" title="Columbia Graduate School of Architecture, Planning and Preservation">Graduate School of Architecture, Planning and Preservation</a> #2,<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> and its <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Graduate_School_of_Journalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbia Graduate School of Journalism">Graduate School of Journalism</a> #1.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Between 1996 and 2008, 18 Columbia affiliates have won Nobel Prizes, of whom nine are faculty members while one is an adjunct senior research scientist (<a href="/wiki/Daniel_Tsui" class="mw-redirect" title="Daniel Tsui">Daniel Tsui</a>) and the other a Global Fellow (<a href="/wiki/Kofi_Annan" title="Kofi Annan">Kofi Annan</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Columbia faculty awarded the Nobel Prize include <a href="/wiki/Richard_Axel" title="Richard Axel">Richard Axel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Chalfie" title="Martin Chalfie">Martin Chalfie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eric_Kandel" title="Eric Kandel">Eric Kandel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tsung-Dao_Lee" title="Tsung-Dao Lee">Tsung-Dao Lee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mundell" title="Robert Mundell">Robert Mundell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orhan_Pamuk" title="Orhan Pamuk">Orhan Pamuk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_S._Phelps" class="mw-redirect" title="Edmund S. Phelps">Edmund S. Phelps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stiglitz" title="Joseph Stiglitz">Joseph Stiglitz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Horst_L._Stormer" class="mw-redirect" title="Horst L. Stormer">Horst L. Stormer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Other awards and honors won by faculty include 30 <a href="/wiki/MacArthur_Foundation" class="mw-redirect" title="MacArthur Foundation">MacArthur Foundation</a> Award winners,<sup id="cite_ref-awards_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-awards-125">[125]</a></sup> 4 <a href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Science" title="National Medal of Science">National Medal of Science</a> recipients,<sup id="cite_ref-awards_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-awards-125">[125]</a></sup> 43 <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Academy_of_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a> Award winners,<sup id="cite_ref-awards_125-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-awards-125">[125]</a></sup> 20 <a href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Engineering" title="National Academy of Engineering">National Academy of Engineering</a> Award winners,<sup id="cite_ref-Members_By_Parent_Institution_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Members_By_Parent_Institution-33">[33]</a></sup> 38 Institute of Medicine of the National Academies Award recipients<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> and 143 <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a> Award winners.<sup id="cite_ref-awards_125-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-awards-125">[125]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 2015, Columbia University was ranked the first in the state by average professor salaries.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> In 2011, the <a href="/wiki/Mines_ParisTech_:_Professional_Ranking_World_Universities" class="mw-redirect" title="Mines ParisTech&#160;: Professional Ranking World Universities">Mines ParisTech&#160;: Professional Ranking World Universities</a> ranked Columbia 3rd best university for forming <a href="/wiki/CEOs" class="mw-redirect" title="CEOs">CEOs</a> in the US and 12th worldwide.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Columbia_University_Court_Yard_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Columbia_University_Court_Yard_01.jpg/220px-Columbia_University_Court_Yard_01.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Columbia_University_Court_Yard_01.jpg/330px-Columbia_University_Court_Yard_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Columbia_University_Court_Yard_01.jpg/440px-Columbia_University_Court_Yard_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Columbia_University_Court_Yard_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nDodge Hall</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Columbia was the first North American site where the <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> atom was split. The College of Physicians and Surgeons played a central role in developing the modern understanding of neuroscience with the publication of <i><a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Neural_Science" title="Principles of Neural Science">Principles of Neural Science</a></i>, described by historian of science Katja Huenther as the "neuroscience \'bible\'".<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> The book was written by a team of Columbia researchers that included Nobel Prize winner <a href="/wiki/Eric_Kandel" title="Eric Kandel">Eric Kandel</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_H._Schwartz_(neurobiologist)" title="James H. Schwartz (neurobiologist)">James H. Schwartz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jessell" title="Thomas Jessell">Thomas Jessell</a>. Columbia was the birthplace of <a href="/wiki/FM_radio" class="mw-redirect" title="FM radio">FM radio</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/MPEG-2" title="MPEG-2">MPEG-2</a> algorithm of transmitting high quality audio and video over limited bandwidth was developed by <a href="/wiki/Dimitris_Anastassiou" title="Dimitris Anastassiou">Dimitris Anastassiou</a>, a Columbia professor of electrical engineering. Biologist <a href="/wiki/Martin_Chalfie" title="Martin Chalfie">Martin Chalfie</a> was the first to introduce the use of <a href="/wiki/Green_Fluorescent_Protein" class="mw-redirect" title="Green Fluorescent Protein">Green Fluorescent Protein</a> (GFP) in labeling cells in intact organisms.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Other inventions and products related to Columbia include Sequential Lateral Solidification (SLS) technology for making LCDs, System Management Arts (SMARTS), <a href="/wiki/Session_Initiation_Protocol" title="Session Initiation Protocol">Session Initiation Protocol</a> (SIP) (which is used for audio, video, chat, instant messaging and whiteboarding), <a href="/wiki/Pharmacopeia" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharmacopeia">pharmacopeia</a>, Macromodel (software for computational chemistry), a new and better recipe for glass concrete, Blue <a href="/wiki/LED" class="mw-redirect" title="LED">LEDs</a>, and Beamprop (used in photonics).<sup id="cite_ref-inventions_nind_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inventions_nind-131">[131]</a></sup> Columbia scientists have been credited with about 175 new inventions in the health sciences each year.<sup id="cite_ref-inventions_nind_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inventions_nind-131">[131]</a></sup> More than 30 pharmaceutical products based on discoveries and inventions made at Columbia are on the market today. These include <a href="/wiki/Remicade" class="mw-redirect" title="Remicade">Remicade</a> (for arthritis), <a href="/wiki/Reopro" class="mw-redirect" title="Reopro">Reopro</a> (for blood clot complications), <a href="/wiki/Xalatan" class="mw-redirect" title="Xalatan">Xalatan</a> (for glaucoma), <a href="/wiki/Benefix" class="mw-redirect" title="Benefix">Benefix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latanoprost" title="Latanoprost">Latanoprost</a> (a glaucoma treatment), shoulder prosthesis, <a href="/wiki/Homocysteine" title="Homocysteine">homocysteine</a> (testing for cardiovascular disease), and <a href="/wiki/Zolinza" class="mw-redirect" title="Zolinza">Zolinza</a> (for cancer therapy).<sup id="cite_ref-inventions_stvss_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inventions_stvss-132">[132]</a></sup> Columbia Technology Ventures (formerly Science and Technology Ventures), as of 2008<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, manages some 600 patents and more than 250 active license agreements.<sup id="cite_ref-inventions_stvss_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inventions_stvss-132">[132]</a></sup> Patent-related deals earned Columbia more than $230&#160;million in the 2006 fiscal year, according to the university, more than any university in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-inventions_pbitctc_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inventions_pbitctc-133">[133]</a></sup> Columbia owns many unique research facilities, such as the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Institute_for_Tele-Information" title="Columbia Institute for Tele-Information">Columbia Institute for Tele-Information</a> dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Telecommunication" title="Telecommunication">telecommunications</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Institute_for_Space_Studies" title="Goddard Institute for Space Studies">Goddard Institute for Space Studies</a>, which is an <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomical</a> <a href="/wiki/Observatory" title="Observatory">observatory</a> affiliated with <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>.</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_life">Student life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Student life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Students">Students</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Students">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table style="float:RIGHT; text-align:center; font-size:85%; margin:auto;" class="wikitable">\n<caption><i>Demographics of student body \xe2\x80\x93 2014</i><sup id="cite_ref-Head_count_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Head_count-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-135">[135]</a></sup></caption>\n<tr>\n<th></th>\n<th>Undergraduate</th>\n<th>Postgraduate</th>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Asian/Pacific Islander</th>\n<td>17.1%</td>\n<td>10.9%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Black</th>\n<td>7.2%</td>\n<td>4.1%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Hispanic</th>\n<td>12.8%</td>\n<td>5.7%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th>American Indian/Alaskan Native</th>\n<td>0.5%</td>\n<td>0.1%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Two or more races</th>\n<td>4.7%</td>\n<td>1.7%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th>White</th>\n<td>38.1%</td>\n<td>32.6%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Non-residents</th>\n<td>13.4%</td>\n<td>34.3%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Unknown</th>\n<td>6.2%</td>\n<td>10.5%</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Walking_Through_Columbia_University_(5892973734).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Walking_Through_Columbia_University_%285892973734%29.jpg/220px-Walking_Through_Columbia_University_%285892973734%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Walking_Through_Columbia_University_%285892973734%29.jpg/330px-Walking_Through_Columbia_University_%285892973734%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Walking_Through_Columbia_University_%285892973734%29.jpg/440px-Walking_Through_Columbia_University_%285892973734%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4368" data-file-height="2912" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Walking_Through_Columbia_University_(5892973734).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nEarl Hall</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>In fall 2014, Columbia University\'s student population was 29,870 (8,559 students in undergraduate programs and 21,311 in postgraduate programs), with 39% of the student population identifying themselves as a minority and 28% born outside of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Head_count_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Head_count-134">[134]</a></sup> Twenty-six percent of students at Columbia have family incomes below $60,000, making it one of the most socioeconomically diverse top-tier colleges.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Sixteen percent of students at Columbia receive Federal Pell Grants,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> which mostly go to students whose family incomes are below $40,000. Fifteen percent of students are the first member of their family to attend a four-year college.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>\n<p>On-campus housing is guaranteed for all four years as an undergraduate. <a href="/wiki/Columbia_College,_Columbia_University" title="Columbia College, Columbia University">Columbia College</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_School_of_Engineering_and_Applied_Science" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbia School of Engineering and Applied Science">Fu Foundation School of Engineering and Applied Science</a> (also known as SEAS or Columbia Engineering) share housing in the on-campus residence halls. First-year students usually live in one of the large residence halls situated around South Lawn: <a href="/wiki/Hartley_Hall" title="Hartley Hall">Hartley Hall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wallach_Hall" title="Wallach Hall">Wallach Hall</a> (originally Livingston Hall), <a href="/wiki/John_Jay_Hall" title="John Jay Hall">John Jay Hall</a>, Furnald Hall or Carman Hall. Upperclassmen participate in a room selection process, wherein students can pick to live in a mix of either corridor- or apartment-style housing with their friends. The <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_School_of_General_Studies" title="Columbia University School of General Studies">Columbia University School of General Studies</a> and graduate schools have their own apartment-style housing in the surrounding neighborhood.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Columbia University is home to many <a href="/wiki/Fraternities_and_sororities_in_North_America" class="mw-redirect" title="Fraternities and sororities in North America">fraternities, sororities</a>, and co-educational Greek organizations. Approximately 10\xe2\x80\x9315% of undergraduate students are associated with Greek life.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> There has been a Greek presence on campus since the establishment in 1836 of the Delta Chapter of <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Delta_Phi" title="Alpha Delta Phi">Alpha Delta Phi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> The InterGreek Council is the self-governing student organization that provides guidelines and support to its member organizations within each of the three councils at Columbia, the Interfraternity Council, Panhellenic Council, and Multicultural Greek Council. The three council presidents bring their affiliated chapters together once a month to meet as one Greek community. The InterGreek Council meetings provide opportunity for member organizations to learn from each other, work together and advocate for community needs.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Publications">Publications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Publications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Columbia_University_Press_logo_(from_Gloria_D%27Amor).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Columbia_University_Press_logo_%28from_Gloria_D%27Amor%29.jpg/220px-Columbia_University_Press_logo_%28from_Gloria_D%27Amor%29.jpg" width="220" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Columbia_University_Press_logo_%28from_Gloria_D%27Amor%29.jpg/330px-Columbia_University_Press_logo_%28from_Gloria_D%27Amor%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Columbia_University_Press_logo_%28from_Gloria_D%27Amor%29.jpg/440px-Columbia_University_Press_logo_%28from_Gloria_D%27Amor%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="616" data-file-height="698" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Columbia_University_Press_logo_(from_Gloria_D%27Amor).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nOne of the earliest logos of <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_Press" title="Columbia University Press">Columbia University Press</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Columbia University is home to a rich diversity of undergraduate, graduate, and professional publications. The <i><a href="/wiki/Columbia_Daily_Spectator" title="Columbia Daily Spectator">Columbia Daily Spectator</a></i> is the nation\'s second-oldest student newspaper;<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Blue_and_White" title="The Blue and White">The Blue and White</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> a monthly literary magazine established in 1890, has recently begun to delve into campus life and local politics in print and on its daily blog, dubbed the <i>Bwog</i>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Morningside_Post" title="The Morningside Post">The Morningside Post</a></i> is a student-run multimedia news publication. Its content: student-written investigative news, international affairs analysis, opinion, and satire.</p>\n<p>Political publications include <i><a href="/wiki/The_Current_(Columbia_University_journal)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Current (Columbia University journal)">The Current</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> a journal of politics, culture and Jewish Affairs; the <i><a href="/wiki/Columbia_Political_Review" title="Columbia Political Review">Columbia Political Review</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> the multi-partisan political magazine of the Columbia Political Union; and <i>AdHoc</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> which denotes itself as the "progressive" campus magazine and deals largely with local political issues and arts events.</p>\n<p>Arts and literary publications include <i>The Columbia Review</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> the nation\'s oldest college literary magazine; <i>Columbia</i>, a nationally regarded <a href="/wiki/Literary_journal" class="mw-redirect" title="Literary journal">literary journal</a>; the <i>Columbia Journal of Literary Criticism</i>;<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> and <i>The Mobius Strip</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> an online arts and literary magazine. <i><a href="/wiki/Inside_New_York" title="Inside New York">Inside New York</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> is an annual guidebook to New York City, written, edited, and published by Columbia undergraduates. Through a distribution agreement with <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_Press" title="Columbia University Press">Columbia University Press</a>, the book is sold at major retailers and independent bookstores.</p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Riverside_Church_from_Columbia_University.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Riverside_Church_from_Columbia_University.jpg/220px-Riverside_Church_from_Columbia_University.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Riverside_Church_from_Columbia_University.jpg/330px-Riverside_Church_from_Columbia_University.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Riverside_Church_from_Columbia_University.jpg/440px-Riverside_Church_from_Columbia_University.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Riverside_Church_from_Columbia_University.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Pupin_Hall" title="Pupin Hall">Pupin Hall</a> (right), <a href="/wiki/Union_Theological_Seminary_in_the_City_of_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Union Theological Seminary in the City of New York">Union Theological Seminary</a> (center) <a href="/wiki/Riverside_Church" title="Riverside Church">Riverside Church</a> (left)</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Columbia is home to numerous undergraduate academic publications. The <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_Politics_%26_Society" title="Journal of Politics &amp; Society">Journal of Politics &amp; Society</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> is a journal of undergraduate research in the social sciences, published and distributed nationally by the <a href="/wiki/Helvidius_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Helvidius Group">Helvidius Group</a>; <i>Publius</i> is an undergraduate journal of politics established in 2008 and published biannually;<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> the <i>Columbia East Asia Review</i> allows undergraduates throughout the world to publish original work on China, Japan, Korea, Tibet, and Vietnam and is supported by the <a href="/wiki/Weatherhead_East_Asian_Institute" title="Weatherhead East Asian Institute">Weatherhead East Asian Institute</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Birch" title="The Birch">The Birch</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> is an undergraduate journal of Eastern European and Eurasian culture that is the first national student-run journal of its kind; the <i>Columbia Political Review</i>, the undergraduate magazine on politics operated by the Columbia Political Union; the <i>Columbia Economics Review</i>, the undergraduate economic journal on research and policy supported by the Columbia Economics Department; and the <i>Columbia Science Review</i> is a science magazine that prints general interest articles, faculty profiles, and student research papers.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>\n<p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Fed_(Columbia_newspaper)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Fed (Columbia newspaper)">The Fed</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> a triweekly satire and investigative newspaper, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Jester_of_Columbia" title="Jester of Columbia">Jester of Columbia</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> the newly (and frequently) revived campus humor magazine both inject humor into local life. Other publications include <i>The Columbian</i>, the undergraduate colleges\' annually published yearbook<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> the <i>Gadfly</i>, a biannual journal of popular philosophy produced by undergraduates;<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> and <i>Rhapsody in Blue</i>, an undergraduate urban studies magazine.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> Professional journals published by academic departments at Columbia University include <i>Current Musicology</i><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Journal_of_Philosophy" title="The Journal of Philosophy">The Journal of Philosophy</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> During the spring semester, graduate students in the Journalism School publish <i>The Bronx Beat</i>, a bi-weekly newspaper covering the South Bronx. Teachers College publishes the <i>Teachers College Record</i>, a journal of research, analysis, and commentary in the field of education, published continuously since 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Founded in 1961 under the auspices of Columbia University\'s Graduate School of Journalism, the <i><a href="/wiki/Columbia_Journalism_Review" title="Columbia Journalism Review">Columbia Journalism Review</a></i> (CJR) examines day-to-day press performance as well as the forces that affect that performance. The magazine is published six times a year, and offers a reporting, analysis, criticism, and commentary. CJR.org, its web site, delivers real-time criticism and reporting, giving CJR a presence in the ongoing conversation about the media.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Broadcasting">Broadcasting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Broadcasting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Earl_Hall_Columbia_University_NYC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Earl_Hall_Columbia_University_NYC.jpg/220px-Earl_Hall_Columbia_University_NYC.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Earl_Hall_Columbia_University_NYC.jpg/330px-Earl_Hall_Columbia_University_NYC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Earl_Hall_Columbia_University_NYC.jpg/440px-Earl_Hall_Columbia_University_NYC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Earl_Hall_Columbia_University_NYC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nEarl Hall</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Columbia is home to two pioneers in undergraduate <a href="/wiki/Campus_radio" title="Campus radio">campus radio</a> broadcasting, WKCR-FM and CTV. <a href="/wiki/WKCR" class="mw-redirect" title="WKCR">WKCR</a>, the student run radio station that broadcasts to the Tri-State area, claims to be the oldest FM radio station in the world, owing to the university\'s affiliation with <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Howard_Armstrong" title="Edwin Howard Armstrong">Major Edwin Armstrong</a>. The station went operational on July 18, 1939, from a 400-foot antenna tower in Alpine, New Jersey, broadcasting the very first FM transmission in the world. Initially, WKCR wasn\'t a radio station, but an organization concerned with the technology of radio communications. As membership grew, however, the nascent club turned its efforts to broadcasting. Armstrong helped the students in their early efforts, donating a microphone and turntables when they designed their first makeshift studio in a dorm room.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> The station has its studios on the second floor of Alfred Lerner Hall on the Morningside campus with its main transmitter tower at <a href="/wiki/4_Times_Square" title="4 Times Square">4 Times Square</a> in <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Manhattan" title="Midtown Manhattan">Midtown Manhattan</a>. Columbia Television (CTV) is the nation\'s second oldest <a href="/wiki/Student_television_station" title="Student television station">Student television station</a> and home of CTV News, a weekly live news program produced by undergraduate students.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Debate_and_Model_UN">Debate and Model UN</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Debate and Model UN">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Philolexian_Society" title="Philolexian Society">Philolexian Society</a> is a literary and debating club founded in 1802, making it the oldest student group at Columbia, as well as the third oldest collegiate literary society in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> The society annually administers the <a href="/wiki/Joyce_Kilmer" title="Joyce Kilmer">Joyce Kilmer</a> Bad Poetry Contest.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> The Columbia Parliamentary Debate Team competes in tournaments around the country as part of the <a href="/wiki/American_Parliamentary_Debate_Association" title="American Parliamentary Debate Association">American Parliamentary Debate Association</a>, and hosts both high school and college tournaments on Columbia\'s campus, as well as public debates on issues affecting the university.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The Columbia International Relations Council and Association (CIRCA), oversees Columbia\'s <a href="/wiki/Model_United_Nations" title="Model United Nations">Model United Nations</a> activities. CIRCA hosts college and high school Model UN conferences, hosts speakers influential in international politics to speak on campus, trains students from underprivileged schools in New York in Model&#160;UN and oversees a competitive team, which travels to colleges around the country and to an international conference every year.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> The competitive team consistently wins best and outstanding delegation awards and is considered one of the top teams in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technology_and_entrepreneurship">Technology and entrepreneurship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Technology and entrepreneurship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pupin_Hall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Pupin_Hall.jpg/220px-Pupin_Hall.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Pupin_Hall.jpg/330px-Pupin_Hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Pupin_Hall.jpg/440px-Pupin_Hall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pupin_Hall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Pupin_Hall" title="Pupin Hall">Pupin Hall</a>, the physics building, showing the rooftop observatory</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CUMathematics11.16.08ByLuigiNovi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/CUMathematics11.16.08ByLuigiNovi.jpg/220px-CUMathematics11.16.08ByLuigiNovi.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/CUMathematics11.16.08ByLuigiNovi.jpg/330px-CUMathematics11.16.08ByLuigiNovi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/CUMathematics11.16.08ByLuigiNovi.jpg/440px-CUMathematics11.16.08ByLuigiNovi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1333" data-file-height="1000" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CUMathematics11.16.08ByLuigiNovi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nMathematics Hall</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The Columbia University Organization of Rising Entrepreneurs (CORE) was founded in 1999. The student-run group aims to foster entrepreneurship on campus. Each year CORE hosts dozens of events, including talks, #StartupColumbia, a conference and venture competition for $250,000, and Ignite@CU, a weekend for undergrads interested in design, engineering, and entrepreneurship. Notable speakers include <a href="/wiki/Peter_Thiel" title="Peter Thiel">Peter Thiel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Dorsey" title="Jack Dorsey">Jack Dorsey</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Ohanian" title="Alexis Ohanian">Alexis Ohanian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drew_Houston" title="Drew Houston">Drew Houston</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Cuban" title="Mark Cuban">Mark Cuban</a>. By 2006, CORE had awarded graduate and undergraduate students over $100,000 in seed capital.</p>\n<p>CampusNetwork, an on-campus social networking site called <a href="/w/index.php?title=Campus_Network&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Campus Network (page does not exist)">Campus Network</a> that preceded Facebook, was created and popularized by Columbia engineering student Adam Goldberg in 2003. <a href="/wiki/Mark_Zuckerberg" title="Mark Zuckerberg">Mark Zuckerberg</a> later asked Goldberg to join him in <a href="/wiki/Palo_Alto" class="mw-redirect" title="Palo Alto">Palo Alto</a> to work on Facebook, but Goldberg declined the offer.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Fu_Foundation_School_of_Engineering_and_Applied_Science" title="Fu Foundation School of Engineering and Applied Science">Fu Foundation School of Engineering and Applied Science</a> offers a minor in Technical Entrepreneurship through its Center for Technology, Innovation, and Community Engagement. SEAS\' entrepreneurship activities focus on community building initiatives in New York and worldwide, made possible through partners such as <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Microsoft Corporation">Microsoft Corporation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Columbia is a top supplier of young engineering entrepreneurs for New York City. Over the past 20 years, graduates of Columbia established over 100 technology companies.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Mayor Bloomberg has provided over $6.7&#160;million towards entrepreneurial programs that partner with Columbia and other universities in New York. Professor Chris Wiggins of the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_School_of_Engineering_and_Applied_Science" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbia School of Engineering and Applied Science">Fu Foundation School of Engineering and Applied Science</a> is working in conjunction with Professors Evan Korth of <a href="/wiki/New_York_University" title="New York University">New York University</a> and Hilary Mason, chief scientist at <a href="/wiki/Bit.ly" class="mw-redirect" title="Bit.ly">bit.ly</a> to facilitate the growth of student tech-startups in an effort to transform a traditionally financially centered New York City into the next <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley" title="Silicon Valley">Silicon Valley</a>. Their website, hackny.org, is a gathering ground of ideas and discussions for New York\'s young entrepreneurial community, the <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Alley" title="Silicon Alley">Silicon Alley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>\n<p>On June 14, 2010, Mayor <a href="/wiki/Michael_R._Bloomberg" class="mw-redirect" title="Michael R. Bloomberg">Michael R. Bloomberg</a> launched the NYC Media Lab to promote innovations in New York\'s media industry.<sup id="cite_ref-NYC20100614_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYC20100614-176">[176]</a></sup> Situated at the <a href="/wiki/New_York_University_Tandon_School_of_Engineering" title="New York University Tandon School of Engineering">New York University Tandon School of Engineering</a>, the lab is a consortium of Columbia University, <a href="/wiki/New_York_University" title="New York University">New York University</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Economic_Development_Corporation" title="New York City Economic Development Corporation">New York City Economic Development Corporation</a> acting to connect companies with universities in new technology research. The Lab is modeled after similar ones at <a href="/wiki/MIT" class="mw-redirect" title="MIT">MIT</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stanford" class="mw-redirect" title="Stanford">Stanford</a>. A $250,000 grant from the New York City Economic Development Corporation was used to establish the NYC Media Lab. Each year, the lab will host a range of roundtable discussions between the private sector and academic institutions. It will support research projects on topics of content format, next-generation search technologies, computer animation for film and gaming, emerging marketing techniques, and new devices development. The lab will also create a media research and development database. Columbia University will coordinate the long-term direction of the media lab as well as the involvement of its faculty and those of other universities.<sup id="cite_ref-NYC20100614_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYC20100614-176">[176]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Athletics">Athletics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Athletics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bigredmarchingband.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Bigredmarchingband.jpg/220px-Bigredmarchingband.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Bigredmarchingband.jpg/330px-Bigredmarchingband.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Bigredmarchingband.jpg/440px-Bigredmarchingband.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bigredmarchingband.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Robert_K._Kraft_Field_at_Lawrence_A._Wien_Stadium" title="Robert K. Kraft Field at Lawrence A. Wien Stadium">Lawrence A. Wien Stadium</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Lions" title="Columbia Lions">Columbia Lions</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Harlem_River#Recreational_uses" title="Harlem River">C-Rock</a></div>\n<p>A member institution of the <a href="/wiki/National_Collegiate_Athletic_Association" title="National Collegiate Athletic Association">National Collegiate Athletic Association</a> (NCAA) in <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Division_I" title="NCAA Division I">Division I</a> <a href="/wiki/Football_Championship_Subdivision" class="mw-redirect" title="Football Championship Subdivision">FCS</a>, Columbia fields varsity teams in 29 sports and is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a>. The football Lions play home games at the 17,000-seat <a href="/wiki/Robert_K._Kraft_Field_at_Lawrence_A._Wien_Stadium" title="Robert K. Kraft Field at Lawrence A. Wien Stadium">Robert K. Kraft Field at Lawrence A. Wien Stadium</a>. The Baker Athletics Complex also includes facilities for baseball, softball, soccer, lacrosse, field hockey, tennis, track and rowing, as well as the new Campbell Sports Center opened in January 2013. The basketball, fencing, swimming &amp; diving, volleyball and wrestling programs are based at the Dodge Physical Fitness Center on the main campus.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Columbia University athletics has a long history, with many accomplishments in athletic fields. In 1870, Columbia played against <a href="/wiki/Rutgers_University" title="Rutgers University">Rutgers University</a> in the second football game in the history of the sport. Eight years later, Columbia crew won the famed <a href="/wiki/Henley_Royal_Regatta" title="Henley Royal Regatta">Henley Royal Regatta</a> in the first-ever defeat for an English crew rowing in English waters. In 1900, Olympian and Columbia College student <a href="/wiki/Maxie_Long" title="Maxie Long">Maxie Long</a> set the first official world record in the 400 meters with a time of 47.8 seconds. In 1983, Columbia men\'s soccer went 18-0 and was ranked first in the nation, but lost to Indiana 1-0 in double overtime in the NCAA championship game; nevertheless, the team went further toward the NCAA title than any Ivy League soccer team in history.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> The football program unfortunately is best known for its record of futility set during the 1980s: between 1983 and 1988, the team lost 44 games in a row, which is still the record for the NCAA <a href="/wiki/Football_Championship_Subdivision" class="mw-redirect" title="Football Championship Subdivision">Football Championship Subdivision</a>. The streak was broken on October 8, 1988, with a 16-13 victory over archrival <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a>. That was the Lions\' first victory at Wien Stadium, which had been opened during the losing streak and was already four years old.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> A new tradition has developed with the Liberty Cup. The Liberty Cup is awarded annually to the winner of the football game between Fordham and Columbia Universities, two of the only three NCAA Division I football teams in New York City. The tradition began in 2002, a year after the Fordham-Columbia game was postponed due to the September 11 attacks.</p>\n<p>Former students include <a href="/wiki/Baseball_Hall_of_Famers" class="mw-redirect" title="Baseball Hall of Famers">Baseball Hall of Famers</a> <a href="/wiki/Lou_Gehrig" title="Lou Gehrig">Lou Gehrig</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Collins" title="Eddie Collins">Eddie Collins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pro_Football_Hall_of_Fame" title="Pro Football Hall of Fame">football Hall of Famer</a> <a href="/wiki/Sid_Luckman" title="Sid Luckman">Sid Luckman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marcellus_Wiley" title="Marcellus Wiley">Marcellus Wiley</a>, and world champion women\'s weightlifter <a href="/wiki/Karyn_Marshall" title="Karyn Marshall">Karyn Marshall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-tws02oct01_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tws02oct01-180">[180]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SI_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SI-181">[181]</a></sup> On May 17, 1939, fledgling <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a> broadcast a doubleheader between the Columbia Lions and the <a href="/wiki/Princeton_Tigers" title="Princeton Tigers">Princeton Tigers</a> at Columbia\'s Baker Field, making it the first televised regular athletic event in history.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_Leaders_Forum">World Leaders Forum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: World Leaders Forum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Uniwersytet_Columbia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Uniwersytet_Columbia.jpg/220px-Uniwersytet_Columbia.jpg" width="220" height="334" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Uniwersytet_Columbia.jpg/330px-Uniwersytet_Columbia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bf/Uniwersytet_Columbia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="395" data-file-height="600" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Uniwersytet_Columbia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nWorld Leaders Forum at <a href="/wiki/Low_Memorial_Library" title="Low Memorial Library">Low Memorial Library</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Established in 2003 by university president <a href="/wiki/Lee_C._Bollinger" class="mw-redirect" title="Lee C. Bollinger">Lee C. Bollinger</a>, the World Leaders Forum at Columbia University provides the opportunity for undergraduate and graduate students alike to listen to world leaders in government, religion, industry, finance, and academia. The World Leaders Forum is a year-around event series that strives to provide a platform for uninhibited speech among nations and cultures, while educating students about problems and progress around the globe.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup></p>\n<p>All Columbia undergraduates and graduates as well as students of <a href="/wiki/Barnard_College" title="Barnard College">Barnard College</a> and other Columbia affiliated schools can register to participate in the World Leaders Forum using their student IDs. Even for individuals who do not have the privilege to attend the event live, they can watch the forum via online videos on Columbia University\'s website.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Past forum speakers include former President of the United States <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>, the Prime Minister of India <a href="/wiki/Atal_Bihari_Vajpayee" title="Atal Bihari Vajpayee">Atal Bihari Vajpayee</a>, Former President of Ghana <a href="/wiki/John_Agyekum_Kufuor" class="mw-redirect" title="John Agyekum Kufuor">John Agyekum Kufuor</a>, President of Afghanistan <a href="/wiki/Hamid_Karzai" title="Hamid Karzai">Hamid Karzai</a>, Prime Minister of Russia <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a>, President of the Republic of Mozambique <a href="/wiki/Joaquim_Alberto_Chissano" class="mw-redirect" title="Joaquim Alberto Chissano">Joaquim Alberto Chissano</a>, President of the Republic of Bolivia <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Diego_Mesa_Gisbert" class="mw-redirect" title="Carlos Diego Mesa Gisbert">Carlos Diego Mesa Gisbert</a>, President of the Republic of Romania <a href="/wiki/Ion_Iliescu" title="Ion Iliescu">Ion Iliescu</a>, President of the Republic of Latvia <a href="/wiki/Vaira_V%C4%AB%C4%B7e-Freiberga" title="Vaira V\xc4\xab\xc4\xb7e-Freiberga">Vaira V\xc4\xab\xc4\xb7e-Freiberga</a>, the first female President of Finland <a href="/wiki/Tarja_Halonen" title="Tarja Halonen">Tarja Halonen</a>, President <a href="/wiki/Yudhoyono" class="mw-redirect" title="Yudhoyono">Yudhoyono</a> of Indonesia, President <a href="/wiki/Pervez_Musharraf" title="Pervez Musharraf">Pervez Musharraf</a> of the Islamic Republic of Pakistan, Iraq President <a href="/wiki/Jalal_Talabani" title="Jalal Talabani">Jalal Talabani</a>, the <a href="/wiki/14th_Dalai_Lama" title="14th Dalai Lama">14th Dalai Lama</a>, President of the Islamic Republic of Iran <a href="/wiki/Mahmoud_Ahmadinejad" title="Mahmoud Ahmadinejad">Mahmoud Ahmadinejad</a>, financier <a href="/wiki/George_Soros" title="George Soros">George Soros</a>, Mayor of New York City <a href="/wiki/Michael_R._Bloomberg" class="mw-redirect" title="Michael R. Bloomberg">Michael R. Bloomberg</a>, President <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A1clav_Klaus" title="V\xc3\xa1clav Klaus">V\xc3\xa1clav Klaus</a> of the Czech Republic, President <a href="/wiki/Cristina_Fern%C3%A1ndez_de_Kirchner" title="Cristina Fern\xc3\xa1ndez de Kirchner">Cristina Fern\xc3\xa1ndez de Kirchner</a> of Argentina, former Secretary-General of the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Kofi_Annan" title="Kofi Annan">Kofi Annan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore" title="Al Gore">Al Gore</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:116th_Street_Columbia_University_Station.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/116th_Street_Columbia_University_Station.JPG/220px-116th_Street_Columbia_University_Station.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/116th_Street_Columbia_University_Station.JPG/330px-116th_Street_Columbia_University_Station.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/116th_Street_Columbia_University_Station.JPG/440px-116th_Street_Columbia_University_Station.JPG 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:116th_Street_Columbia_University_Station.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nAccess to Columbia is enhanced by the <a href="/wiki/116th_Street_%E2%80%93_Columbia_University_(IRT_Broadway_%E2%80%93_Seventh_Avenue_Line)" class="mw-redirect" title="116th Street \xe2\x80\x93 Columbia University (IRT Broadway \xe2\x80\x93 Seventh Avenue Line)">116th Street \xe2\x80\x93 Columbia University</a> subway station (<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/1_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" title="1 (New York City Subway service)">1</a></span>&#160;train) on the <a href="/wiki/IRT_Broadway_%E2%80%93_Seventh_Avenue_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="IRT Broadway \xe2\x80\x93 Seventh Avenue Line">IRT Broadway \xe2\x80\x93 Seventh Avenue Line</a>.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The Columbia University Orchestra was founded by composer Edward MacDowell in 1896, and is the oldest continually operating university orchestra in the United States. Undergraduate student composers at Columbia may choose to become involved with Columbia New Music, which sponsors concerts of music written by undergraduate students from all of Columbia\'s schools.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup></p>\n<p>There are a number of performing arts groups at Columbia dedicated to producing student theater, including the Columbia Players, King\'s Crown Shakespeare Troupe (KCST), Columbia Musical Theater Society (CMTS), NOMADS (New and Original Material Authored and Directed by Students), LateNite Theatre, Columbia University Performing Arts League (CUPAL), Black Theatre Ensemble (BTE), sketch comedy group Chowdah, and improvisational troupes Alfred and Fruit Paunch.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_Marching_Band" title="Columbia University Marching Band">Columbia University Marching Band</a> tells jokes during the campus tradition of Orgo Night.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Le_Penseur_at_Columbia_University_(6435262909).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Le_Penseur_at_Columbia_University_%286435262909%29.jpg/220px-Le_Penseur_at_Columbia_University_%286435262909%29.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Le_Penseur_at_Columbia_University_%286435262909%29.jpg/330px-Le_Penseur_at_Columbia_University_%286435262909%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Le_Penseur_at_Columbia_University_%286435262909%29.jpg/440px-Le_Penseur_at_Columbia_University_%286435262909%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1063" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Le_Penseur_at_Columbia_University_(6435262909).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<i><a href="/wiki/The_Thinker" title="The Thinker">The Thinker</a></i> (<i>Le Penseur</i>) at Columbia University</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Queer_Alliance" title="Columbia Queer Alliance">Columbia Queer Alliance</a> is the central Columbia student organization that represents the bisexual, lesbian, gay, transgender, and questioning student population. It is the oldest gay student organization in the world, founded as the Student <a href="/wiki/Homophile" title="Homophile">Homophile</a> League in 1967 by students including lifelong activist <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Donaldson_(activist)" title="Stephen Donaldson (activist)">Stephen Donaldson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> Columbia University campus military groups include the U.S. Military Veterans of Columbia University and Advocates for Columbia ROTC. In the 2005\xe2\x80\x9306 academic year, the Columbia Military Society, Columbia\'s student group for ROTC cadets and Marine officer candidates, was renamed the Hamilton Society for "students who aspire to serve their nation through the military in the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The university also houses an independent nonprofit organization, Community Impact, which strives to serve disadvantaged people in the Harlem, Washington Heights, and Morningside Heights communities. From its earliest inception as a single service initiative formed in 1981 by Columbia University undergraduates, Community Impact has grown into Columbia University\'s largest student service organization. CI provides food, clothing, shelter, education, job training, and companionship for residents in its surrounding communities. CI consists of a dedicated corps of about 950 Columbia University student volunteers participating in 25 community service programs, which serve more than 8,000 people each year.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_activism">Student activism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Student activism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protests_of_1968">Protests of 1968</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Protests of 1968">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hamilton_statue_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0958.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Hamilton_statue_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0958.JPG/220px-Hamilton_statue_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0958.JPG" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Hamilton_statue_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0958.JPG/330px-Hamilton_statue_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0958.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Hamilton_statue_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0958.JPG/440px-Hamilton_statue_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0958.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3831" data-file-height="2757" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hamilton_statue_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0958.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Hall_(Columbia_University)" title="Hamilton Hall (Columbia University)">Hamilton Hall</a> was occupied by protesting students in 1968</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_protests_of_1968" title="Columbia University protests of 1968">Columbia University protests of 1968</a></div>\n<p>Students initiated a major demonstration in 1968 over two main issues. The first was Columbia\'s proposed gymnasium in neighboring <a href="/wiki/Morningside_Park_(New_York_City)" title="Morningside Park (New York City)">Morningside Park</a>; this was seen by the protesters to be an act of aggression aimed at the black residents of neighboring <a href="/wiki/Harlem" title="Harlem">Harlem</a>. A second issue was the Columbia administration\'s failure to resign its institutional membership in <a href="/wiki/The_Pentagon" title="The Pentagon">the Pentagon</a>\'s weapons research think-tank, the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Defense_Analyses" title="Institute for Defense Analyses">Institute for Defense Analyses</a> (IDA). Students barricaded themselves inside <a href="/wiki/Low_Memorial_Library" title="Low Memorial Library">Low Library</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Hall_(Columbia_University)" title="Hamilton Hall (Columbia University)">Hamilton Hall</a>, and several other university buildings during the protests, and New York City police were called onto the campus to arrest or forcibly remove the students.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The protests achieved two of their stated goals. Columbia disaffiliated from the IDA and scrapped the plans for the controversial gym, building a subterranean physical fitness center under the north end of campus instead. A popular myth states that the gym\'s plans were eventually used by <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a> for the expansion of its athletic facilities, but as <a href="/wiki/Jadwin_Gymnasium" title="Jadwin Gymnasium">Jadwin Gymnasium</a> was already 50% complete by 1966 (when the Columbia gym was announced) this was clearly not correct.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> At least 30 Columbia students were suspended by the administration as a result of the protests. Many of the Class of \'68 walked out of their graduation and held a countercommencement on Low Plaza with a picnic following at Morningside Park, the place where the protests began.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> The protests hurt Columbia financially as many potential students chose to attend other universities and some alumni refused to donate money to the school. <a href="/wiki/Allan_Bloom" title="Allan Bloom">Allan Bloom</a>, a professor of philosophy at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a>, believed that the protest efforts at Columbia were responsible for pushing higher education further toward the liberal left. As a result of the protests, Bloom stated, "American universities were no longer places of intellectual and academic debate, but rather places of \'political correctness\' and liberalism."<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (January 2013)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protests_against_racism_and_apartheid">Protests against racism and apartheid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Protests against racism and apartheid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Columbia_University_by_Rangilo.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Columbia_University_by_Rangilo.JPG/220px-Columbia_University_by_Rangilo.JPG" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Columbia_University_by_Rangilo.JPG/330px-Columbia_University_by_Rangilo.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Columbia_University_by_Rangilo.JPG/440px-Columbia_University_by_Rangilo.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="4608" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Columbia_University_by_Rangilo.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nSchool of Arts</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Further student protests, including hunger strike and more barricades of <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Hall_(Columbia_University)" title="Hamilton Hall (Columbia University)">Hamilton Hall</a> and the Business School<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> during the late 1970s and early 1980s, were aimed at convincing the university trustees to divest all of the university\'s investments in companies that were seen as active or tacit supporters of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_South_Africa_in_the_apartheid_era" class="mw-redirect" title="History of South Africa in the apartheid era">apartheid</a> regime in South Africa. A notable upsurge in the protests occurred in 1978, when following a celebration of the tenth anniversary of the student uprising in 1968, students marched and rallied in protest of university investments in South Africa. The Committee Against Investment in South Africa (CAISA) and numerous student groups including the Socialist Action Committee, the Black Student Organization and the Gay Students group joined together and succeeded in pressing for the first partial divestment of a U.S. university.</p>\n<p>The initial (and partial) Columbia divestment,<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> focused largely on bonds and financial institutions directly involved with the South African regime.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> It followed a year-long campaign first initiated by students who had worked together to block the appointment of former <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">United States Secretary of State</a> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a> to an endowed chair at the university in 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Broadly backed by student groups and many faculty members the Committee Against Investment in South Africa held teach-ins and demonstrations through the year focused on the trustees ties to the corporations doing business with South Africa. Trustee meetings were picketed and interrupted by demonstrations culminating in May 1978 in the takeover of the Graduate School of Business.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ahmadinejad_speech_controversy">Ahmadinejad speech controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Ahmadinejad speech controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mahmoud_Ahmadinejad_at_Columbia_13_by_David_Shankbone.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Mahmoud_Ahmadinejad_at_Columbia_13_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/220px-Mahmoud_Ahmadinejad_at_Columbia_13_by_David_Shankbone.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Mahmoud_Ahmadinejad_at_Columbia_13_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/330px-Mahmoud_Ahmadinejad_at_Columbia_13_by_David_Shankbone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Mahmoud_Ahmadinejad_at_Columbia_13_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/440px-Mahmoud_Ahmadinejad_at_Columbia_13_by_David_Shankbone.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mahmoud_Ahmadinejad_at_Columbia_13_by_David_Shankbone.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nStudents protest Ahmadinejad\'s invitation to speak at Columbia University</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/School_of_International_and_Public_Affairs" class="mw-redirect" title="School of International and Public Affairs">School of International and Public Affairs</a> extends invitations to heads of state and heads of government who come to New York City for the opening of the fall session of the United Nations General Assembly. In 2007, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iranian</a> President <a href="/wiki/Mahmoud_Ahmadinejad" title="Mahmoud Ahmadinejad">Mahmoud Ahmadinejad</a> was one of those invited to speak on campus. Ahmadinejad accepted his invitation and spoke on September 24, 2007, as part of Columbia University\'s World Leaders Forum.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> The invitation proved to be highly controversial. Hundreds of demonstrators swarmed the campus on September 24 and the speech itself was televised worldwide. University President <a href="/wiki/Lee_C._Bollinger" class="mw-redirect" title="Lee C. Bollinger">Lee C. Bollinger</a> tried to allay the controversy by letting Ahmadenijad speak, but with a negative introduction (given personally by Bollinger). This did not mollify those who were displeased with the fact that the Iranian leader had been invited onto the campus.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> Columbia students, though, turned out en masse to listen to the speech on the South Lawn. An estimated 2,500 undergraduates and graduates came out for the historic occasion.</p>\n<p>During his speech, Ahmadinejad criticized <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>\'s policies towards the <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Palestinian people">Palestinians</a>; called for research on the historical accuracy of <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a>; raised questions as to who initiated the <a href="/wiki/September_11,_2001_attacks" class="mw-redirect" title="September 11, 2001 attacks">9/11 attacks</a>; defended Iran\'s <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_program_of_Iran" title="Nuclear program of Iran">nuclear power program</a>, criticizing the UN\'s policy of sanctions on his country; and attacked <a href="/wiki/Foreign_policy_of_the_United_States" title="Foreign policy of the United States">U.S. foreign policy</a> in the Middle East. In response to a question about Iran\'s <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Iran" title="Human rights in Iran">treatment</a> of <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights_in_Iran" title="Women\'s rights in Iran">women</a> and <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Iran" title="LGBT rights in Iran">homosexuals</a>, he asserted that women are respected in Iran and that "In Iran, we don\'t have homosexuals like in your country... In Iran, we do not have this phenomenon. I don\'t know who told you this."<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> The latter statement drew laughter from the audience. The Manhattan District Attorney\'s Office accused Columbia of accepting grant money from the <a href="/wiki/Alavi_Foundation" title="Alavi Foundation">Alavi Foundation</a> to support faculty "sympathetic" to Iran\'s Islamic republic.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="ROTC_controversy">ROTC controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: ROTC controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Beginning in 1969, during the Vietnam War, the university did not allow the U.S. military to have <a href="/wiki/Reserve_Officers%27_Training_Corps" title="Reserve Officers\' Training Corps">Reserve Officers\' Training Corps</a> (ROTC) programs on campus,<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> though Columbia students could participate in ROTC programs at other local colleges and universities.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-home.manhattan.edu_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-home.manhattan.edu-212">[212]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> At a forum at the university during the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2008" title="United States presidential election, 2008">2008 presidential election campaign</a>, both <a href="/wiki/John_McCain" title="John McCain">John McCain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> said that the university should consider reinstating ROTC on campus.<sup id="cite_ref-home.manhattan.edu_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-home.manhattan.edu-212">[212]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> After the debate, the President of the University, <a href="/wiki/Lee_C._Bollinger" class="mw-redirect" title="Lee C. Bollinger">Lee C. Bollinger</a>, stated that he did not favor reinstating Columbia\'s ROTC program, because of the military\'s anti-gay policies. In November 2008, Columbia\'s undergraduate student body held a referendum on the question of whether or not to invite ROTC back to campus, and the students who voted were almost evenly divided on the issue. ROTC lost the vote (which would not have been binding on the administration, and did not include graduate students, faculty, or alumni) by a fraction of a percentage point.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>\n<p>In April 2010 during <a href="/wiki/Admiral" title="Admiral">Admiral</a> <a href="/wiki/Mike_Mullen" class="mw-redirect" title="Mike Mullen">Mike Mullen</a>\'s address at Columbia, President <a href="/wiki/Lee_C._Bollinger" class="mw-redirect" title="Lee C. Bollinger">Lee C. Bollinger</a> stated that the ROTC would be readmitted to campus if the admiral\'s plans for revoking the <a href="/wiki/Don%27t_ask,_don%27t_tell" title="Don\'t ask, don\'t tell">don\'t ask, don\'t tell</a> policy were successful. In February 2011 during one of three town-hall meetings on the ROTC ban, former Army staff sergeant Anthony Maschek, a <a href="/wiki/Purple_Heart" title="Purple Heart">Purple Heart</a> recipient for injuries sustained during his service in Iraq, was booed and hissed at by some students during his speech promoting the idea of allowing the ROTC on campus.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> In April 2011 the Columbia University Senate voted to welcome the ROTC program back on campus.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> Secretary of the Navy <a href="/wiki/Ray_Mabus" title="Ray Mabus">Ray Mabus</a> and Columbia University President Lee C. Bollinger signed an agreement to reinstate <a href="/wiki/Naval_Reserve_Officers_Training_Corps" title="Naval Reserve Officers Training Corps">Naval Reserve Officers Training Corps</a> (NROTC) program at Columbia for the first time in more than 40 years on May 26, 2011. The agreement was signed at a ceremony on board the <a href="/wiki/USS_Iwo_Jima" title="USS Iwo Jima">USS Iwo Jima</a>, docked in New York for the Navy\'s annual Fleet Week.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditions">Traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Traditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Columbia_traditions" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbia traditions">Columbia traditions</a>.</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orgo_Night">Orgo Night</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Orgo Night">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>From 1975 until 2016, on the day before the Organic Chemistry exam\xe2\x80\x94which is often on the first day of finals\xe2\x80\x94at precisely the stroke of midnight, the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_Marching_Band" title="Columbia University Marching Band">Columbia University Marching Band</a> occupied <a href="/wiki/Butler_Library" title="Butler Library">Butler Library</a> to distract diligent students from studying. After a forty-five minutes or so of jokes and music, the procession then moved out to the lawn in front of Hartley, Wallach and John Jay residence halls to entertain the residents there. The band then plays at other locations around Morningside Heights, including the residential quadrangle of <a href="/wiki/Barnard_College" title="Barnard College">Barnard College</a>, where students of the all-women\'s school, in mock-consternation, rain trash \xe2\x80\x93 including notes and course packets \xe2\x80\x93 and water balloons upon them from their dormitories above. The band tends to close their Orgo Night performances before Furnald Hall, known among students as the more studious and reportedly "anti-social" residence hall, where the underclassmen in the band serenade the graduating seniors with an entertaining, though vulgar, mock-hymn to Columbia, composed of quips that poke fun at the stereotypes about the Columbia student body.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In December 2016, University administrators banned the Marching Band from performing its Orgo Night show in its traditional Butler Library location, setting off a storm of protests and accusations that University President Lee C.
